	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Mona Lisa</i></h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the painting. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Mona Lisa (disambiguation)">Mona Lisa (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Painting by Leonardo da Vinci</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<table class="infobox vevent" style="width:22em;width: 22em; line-height: 1.4em; text-align: left; float:right;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="summary" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size: 125%; font-weight: bold; font-style: italic">Mona Lisa</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i><i lang="it" title="Italian language text">Monna Lisa</i></i>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i><i lang="it" title="Italian language text">La Gioconda</i></i></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa,_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci,_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg" class="image" title="See adjacent text."><img alt="See adjacent text." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/300px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="447" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/450px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/600px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7479" data-file-height="11146" /></a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em;">Artist</th><td class="attendee"><a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em;">Year</th><td><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1503–1506, perhaps continuing until <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1517</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em;">Medium</th><td class="category"><a href="/wiki/Oil_painting" title="Oil painting">Oil</a> on <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a> panel</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em;">Subject</th><td><a href="/wiki/Lisa_del_Giocondo" title="Lisa del Giocondo">Lisa Gherardini</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em;">Dimensions</th><td>77&#160;cm&#32;×&#160;53&#160;cm&#32;(30&#160;in&#32;×&#160;21&#160;in)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.65em;">Location</th><td><a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">The Louvre Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i><b>Mona Lisa</b></i> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i lang="it"><b>Monna Lisa</b></i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[ˈmɔnna ˈliːza]</a></span> or <i lang="it" title="Italian language text"><b>La Gioconda</b></i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[la dʒoˈkonda]</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr"><b>La Joconde</b></i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[la ʒɔkɔ̃d]</a></span>) is a half-length <a href="/wiki/Portrait_painting" title="Portrait painting">portrait painting</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> that has been described as "the best known, the most visited, the most written about, the most sung about, the most parodied work of art in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Mona Lisa</i> is also one of the most valuable paintings in the world. It holds the <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Record</a> for the highest known insurance valuation in history at US$100&#160;million in 1962<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> (equivalent to $650 million in 2018).<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The painting is thought by many to be a portrait of <a href="/wiki/Lisa_del_Giocondo" title="Lisa del Giocondo">Lisa Gherardini</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> the wife of Francesco del Giocondo, and is in oil on a white <a href="/wiki/Populus_nigra" title="Populus nigra">Lombardy poplar</a> <a href="/wiki/Panel_painting" title="Panel painting">panel</a>. It had been believed to have been painted between 1503 and 1506; however, Leonardo may have continued working on it as late as 1517. Recent academic work suggests that it would not have been started before 1513.<sup id="cite_ref-Pedretti_1982_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pedretti_1982-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vezzosi_2007_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vezzosi_2007-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lorusso_Natali_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorusso_Natali-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asmus_Parfenov_Elford_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asmus_Parfenov_Elford-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It was acquired by <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">King Francis I of France</a> and is now the property of the <a href="/wiki/French_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="French Republic">French Republic</a>, on permanent display at the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> since 1797.<sup id="cite_ref-carrier_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carrier-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The subject's expression, which is frequently described as enigmatic,<sup id="cite_ref-ns1_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ns1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> the monumentality of the composition, the subtle modelling of forms, and the atmospheric illusionism were novel qualities that have contributed to the continuing fascination and study of the work.<sup id="cite_ref-Louvre_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Louvre-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_and_subject">Title and subject</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lisa_del_Giocondo" title="Lisa del Giocondo">Lisa del Giocondo</a></div>
<p>The title of the painting, which is known in English as <i>Mona Lisa</i>, comes from a description by <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> art historian <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a>, who wrote "Leonardo undertook to paint, for Francesco del Giocondo, the portrait of Mona Lisa, his wife."<sup id="cite_ref-Vasari_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vasari-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <i lang="it" title="Italian language text">Mona</i> in Italian is a polite form of address originating as <i lang="it" title="Italian language text">ma donna</i> – similar to "Ma'am", "Madam", or "<a href="/wiki/My_lady" class="mw-redirect" title="My lady">my lady</a>" in English. This became <i lang="it" title="Italian language text"><a href="/wiki/Madonna_(art)" title="Madonna (art)">madonna</a></i>, and its contraction <i lang="it" title="Italian language text">mona</i>. The title of the painting, though traditionally spelled "Mona" (as used by Vasari),<sup id="cite_ref-Vasari_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vasari-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> is also commonly spelled in modern Italian as <i>Monna Lisa</i> ("<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mona#Italian" class="extiw" title="wikt:mona">mona</a>" being a vulgarity in some Italian dialects), but this is rare in English.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Vasari's account of the <i>Mona Lisa</i> comes from his biography of Leonardo published in 1550, 31 years after the artist's death. It has long been the best-known source of information on the <a href="/wiki/Provenance" title="Provenance">provenance</a> of the work and identity of the sitter. Leonardo's assistant <a href="/wiki/Sala%C3%AC" title="Salaì">Salaì</a>, at his death in 1524, owned a portrait which in his personal papers was named <i>la Gioconda</i>, a painting bequeathed to him by Leonardo.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:382px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_margin_scribble.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mona_Lisa_margin_scribble.jpg/380px-Mona_Lisa_margin_scribble.jpg" decoding="async" width="380" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mona_Lisa_margin_scribble.jpg/570px-Mona_Lisa_margin_scribble.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mona_Lisa_margin_scribble.jpg/760px-Mona_Lisa_margin_scribble.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_margin_scribble.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A margin note by <a href="/wiki/Agostino_Vespucci" title="Agostino Vespucci">Agostino Vespucci</a> (visible at right) discovered in a book at <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University" title="Heidelberg University">Heidelberg University</a>. Dated 1503, it states that Leonardo was working on a portrait of <a href="/wiki/Lisa_del_Giocondo" title="Lisa del Giocondo">Lisa del Giocondo</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>That Leonardo painted such a work, and its date, were confirmed in 2005 when a scholar at <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University" title="Heidelberg University">Heidelberg University</a> discovered a marginal note in a 1477 printing of a volume by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Roman</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>. Dated October 1503, the note was written by Leonardo's contemporary <a href="/wiki/Agostino_Vespucci" title="Agostino Vespucci">Agostino Vespucci</a>. This note likens Leonardo to renowned Greek painter <a href="/wiki/Apelles" title="Apelles">Apelles</a>, who is mentioned in the text, and states that Leonardo was at that time working on a painting of Lisa del Giocondo.<sup id="cite_ref-subject_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-subject-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In response to the announcement of the discovery of this document, Vincent Delieuvin, the Louvre representative, stated "Leonardo da Vinci was painting, in 1503, the portrait of a Florentine lady by the name of Lisa del Giocondo. About this we are now certain. Unfortunately, we cannot be absolutely certain that this portrait of Lisa del Giocondo is the painting of the Louvre."<sup id="cite_ref-Vincent_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Model_(art)" title="Model (art)">model</a>, Lisa del Giocondo,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
was a member of the Gherardini family of <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>, and the wife of wealthy Florentine silk merchant Francesco del Giocondo.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The painting is thought to have been commissioned for their new home, and to celebrate the birth of their second son, Andrea.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The Italian name for the painting, <i lang="it" title="Italian language text">La Gioconda</i>, means 'jocund' ('happy' or 'jovial') or, literally, 'the jocund one', a pun on the feminine form of Lisa's married name, Giocondo.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-louvre1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-louvre1-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In French, the title <i lang="fr" title="French language text">La Joconde</i> has the same meaning.
</p><p>Before that discovery, scholars had developed several <a href="/wiki/Speculations_about_Mona_Lisa" title="Speculations about Mona Lisa">alternative views</a> as to the subject of the painting. Some argued that Lisa del Giocondo was the subject of a different portrait, identifying at least four other paintings as the <i>Mona Lisa</i> referred to by Vasari.<sup id="cite_ref-monna_bella_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monna_bella-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Littlefield_525_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Littlefield_525-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
Several other women have been proposed as the subject of the painting.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_Aragon,_Duchess_of_Milan" title="Isabella of Aragon, Duchess of Milan">Isabella of Aragon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Debelle_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Debelle-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cecilia_Gallerani" title="Cecilia Gallerani">Cecilia Gallerani</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Johnston_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Costanza_d%27Avalos,_Duchess_of_Francavilla" title="Costanza d&#39;Avalos, Duchess of Francavilla">Costanza d'Avalos, Duchess of Francavilla</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isabella_d%27Este" title="Isabella d&#39;Este">Isabella d'Este</a>, Pacifica Brandano or Brandino, Isabela Gualanda, <a href="/wiki/Caterina_Sforza" title="Caterina Sforza">Caterina Sforza</a>—even Salaì and Leonardo himself—are all among the list of posited models portrayed in the painting.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-Faces_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-Faces-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-myth_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-myth-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
The consensus of art historians in the 21st century maintains the long-held traditional opinion that the painting depicts Lisa del Giocondo.<sup id="cite_ref-subject_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-subject-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>Leonardo da Vinci had begun working on a portrait of <a href="/wiki/Lisa_del_Giocondo" title="Lisa del Giocondo">Lisa del Giocondo</a>, the model of the <i>Mona Lisa</i>, by October 1503.<sup id="cite_ref-subject_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-subject-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vincent_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> It is believed by some that the <i>Mona Lisa</i> was begun in 1503 or 1504 in Florence.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Although the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> states that it was "doubtless painted between 1503 and 1506",<sup id="cite_ref-Louvre_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Louvre-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> art historian <a href="/wiki/Martin_Kemp_(art_historian)" title="Martin Kemp (art historian)">Martin Kemp</a> says that there are some difficulties in confirming the dates with certainty.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In addition, many Leonardo experts, such as <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Pedretti" title="Carlo Pedretti">Carlo Pedretti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Vezzosi" title="Alessandro Vezzosi">Alessandro Vezzosi</a>, are of the opinion that the painting is characteristic of Leonardo's style in the final years of his life, post-1513.<sup id="cite_ref-Pedretti_1982_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pedretti_1982-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vezzosi_2007_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vezzosi_2007-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Other academics argue that, given the historical documentation, Leonardo would have painted the work from 1513.<sup id="cite_ref-Asmus_Parfenov_Elford_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asmus_Parfenov_Elford-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> According to Vasari, "after he had lingered over it four years, [he] left it unfinished".<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In 1516, Leonardo was invited by <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">King Francis I</a> to work at the <a href="/wiki/Clos_Luc%C3%A9" title="Clos Lucé">Clos Lucé</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_d%27Amboise" title="Château d&#39;Amboise">Château d'Amboise</a>; it is believed that he took the <i>Mona Lisa</i> with him and continued to work on it after he moved to France.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-Faces_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-Faces-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Art historian Carmen C. Bambach has concluded that Leonardo probably continued refining the work until 1516 or 1517.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Leonardo's right hand was paralytic circa 1517,<sup id="cite_ref-seeker_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seeker-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> which may indicate why he left the <i>Mona Lisa</i> unfinished.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian2005_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian2005-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fainting_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fainting-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Raffaello_Sanzio_-_Portrait_of_a_Woman_-_WGA18948.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Raffaello_Sanzio_-_Portrait_of_a_Woman_-_WGA18948.jpg/220px-Raffaello_Sanzio_-_Portrait_of_a_Woman_-_WGA18948.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Raffaello_Sanzio_-_Portrait_of_a_Woman_-_WGA18948.jpg/330px-Raffaello_Sanzio_-_Portrait_of_a_Woman_-_WGA18948.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Raffaello_Sanzio_-_Portrait_of_a_Woman_-_WGA18948.jpg/440px-Raffaello_Sanzio_-_Portrait_of_a_Woman_-_WGA18948.jpg 2x" data-file-width="717" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Raffaello_Sanzio_-_Portrait_of_a_Woman_-_WGA18948.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>'s drawing (c. 1505), after Leonardo;<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> today in the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> along with the <i>Mona Lisa</i><sup id="cite_ref-Reynal_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynal-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Circa 1504, <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> executed a pen-and-ink sketch, in which the subject is flanked by large columns. Experts universally agree that it is based on Leonardo's portrait.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lorusso_Natali_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorusso_Natali-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Isbouts_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Isbouts-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Other later copies of the <i>Mona Lisa</i>, such as those in the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Art,_Architecture_and_Design" title="National Museum of Art, Architecture and Design">National Museum of Art, Architecture and Design</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Walters_Art_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="The Walters Art Museum">The Walters Art Museum</a>, also display large flanking columns. As a result, it was thought that the <i>Mona Lisa</i> had once had side columns and been trimmed.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> However, by 1993, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Z%C3%B6llner" title="Frank Zöllner">Frank Zöllner</a> observed that the painting surface had never been trimmed;<sup id="cite_ref-Zollner_1993_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zollner_1993-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> this was confirmed through a series of tests in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-mohen_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohen-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In view of this, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vincent_Delieuvin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vincent Delieuvin (page does not exist)">Vincent Delieuvin</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vincent_Delieuvin" class="extiw" title="fr:Vincent Delieuvin">fr</a>&#93;</span>, curator of 16th-century Italian painting at the Louvre, states that the sketch and these other copies must have been inspired by another version,<sup id="cite_ref-Delieuvin_Tallec_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delieuvin_Tallec-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> while Zöllner states that the sketch may be after another Leonardo portrait of the same subject.<sup id="cite_ref-Zollner_1993_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zollner_1993-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Vasari, the painting was painted for the model's husband, Francesco del Giocondo.<sup id="cite_ref-Vasari2_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vasari2-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The record of an October 1517 visit by <a href="/wiki/Luigi_d%27Aragona" title="Luigi d&#39;Aragona">Louis d'Aragon</a> states that it was executed for the deceased <a href="/wiki/Giuliano_di_Lorenzo_de%27_Medici" class="mw-redirect" title="Giuliano di Lorenzo de&#39; Medici">Giuliano de' Medici</a>, Leonardo's steward at the <a href="/wiki/Belvedere_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Belvedere Palace">Belvedere Palace</a> between 1513 and 1516,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> but this was likely an error.<sup id="cite_ref-Wallace_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> A number of experts have argued that Leonardo made two versions of the portrait (because of the conflicting dating, the presence of flanking columns in Raphael's sketch, and the uncertainty concerning its commissioner—as well as its fate following Leonardo's death in 1519).<sup id="cite_ref-Isbouts_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Isbouts-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lorusso_Natali_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorusso_Natali-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boudin_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boudin-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The first, containing flanking columns, would have been commissioned by Giocondo circa 1503, and left unfinished in Leonardo's pupil and assistant <a href="/wiki/Sala%C3%AC" title="Salaì">Salaì</a>'s possession until his death in 1524. The second, commissioned by Giuliano de' Medici circa 1513, would have been sold by Salaì to Francis I in 1518<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> and is the one in the Louvre today.<sup id="cite_ref-Isbouts_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Isbouts-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lorusso_Natali_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorusso_Natali-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boudin_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boudin-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
Other believe that there was only one true <i>Mona Lisa</i>, but are divided as to the two aforementioned fates.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_Pallanti_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp_Pallanti-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
The famed painting was kept at the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Fontainebleau" title="Palace of Fontainebleau">Palace of Fontainebleau</a>, where it remained until <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a> moved it to the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Palace of Versailles</a>, where it remained until the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 1797, it went on permanent display at the Louvre.<sup id="cite_ref-carrier_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carrier-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 21st century, French scientist Pascal Cotte hypothesized a hidden portrait underneath the surface of the painting, <a href="/wiki/Circumstantial_evidence" title="Circumstantial evidence">circumstantial evidence</a> for which was produced using reflective light technology.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cotte_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cotte-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The underlying portrait appears to be of a model looking to the side, but lacks the flanking columns drawn by Raphael.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Having been given access to the painting by the Louvre in 2004, Cotte spent ten years studying the painting with layer-amplification methods.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apollo_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apollo-62">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
However, the alleged portrait does not fit with historical descriptions of the painting: both Vasari and <a href="/wiki/Gian_Paolo_Lomazzo" title="Gian Paolo Lomazzo">Gian Paolo Lomazzo</a> describe the subject as smiling,<sup id="cite_ref-Vasari_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vasari-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> unlike the subject in Cotte's portrait. Cotte admits that his reconstitution had been carried out only in support of his hypotheses and should not be considered as objective proof of an underlying portrait.<sup id="cite_ref-Cotte_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cotte-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_Pallanti_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp_Pallanti-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Refuge.2C_theft_and_vandalism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Refuge,_theft_and_vandalism">Refuge, theft and vandalism</span></h3>
<p>After the French Revolution, the painting was moved to the Louvre, but spent a brief period in the bedroom of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> (d.&#160;1821) in the <a href="/wiki/Tuileries_Palace" title="Tuileries Palace">Tuileries Palace</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <i>Mona Lisa</i> was not widely known outside the art world, but in the 1860s, a portion of the French intelligentsia began to hail it as a masterwork of Renaissance painting.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
During the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a> (1870–1871), the painting was moved from the Louvre to the <a href="/wiki/Brest_Arsenal" title="Brest Arsenal">Brest Arsenal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bohm-Duchen2001p53_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohm-Duchen2001p53-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, it was again removed from the Louvre and taken first to the Château d'Amboise, then to the <a href="/wiki/Loc-Dieu_Abbey" title="Loc-Dieu Abbey">Loc-Dieu Abbey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Chambord" title="Château de Chambord">Château de Chambord</a>, then finally to the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Ingres" title="Musée Ingres">Ingres Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Montauban" title="Montauban">Montauban</a>.
By 1911, the painting was still not popular among the lay-public.<sup id="cite_ref-ny2019_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ny2019-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_Found,_La_Joconde_est_Retrouv%C3%A9e,_Le_Petit_Parisien,_Num%C3%A9ro_13559,_13_December_1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Mona_Lisa_Found%2C_La_Joconde_est_Retrouv%C3%A9e%2C_Le_Petit_Parisien%2C_Num%C3%A9ro_13559%2C_13_December_1913.jpg/170px-Mona_Lisa_Found%2C_La_Joconde_est_Retrouv%C3%A9e%2C_Le_Petit_Parisien%2C_Num%C3%A9ro_13559%2C_13_December_1913.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Mona_Lisa_Found%2C_La_Joconde_est_Retrouv%C3%A9e%2C_Le_Petit_Parisien%2C_Num%C3%A9ro_13559%2C_13_December_1913.jpg/255px-Mona_Lisa_Found%2C_La_Joconde_est_Retrouv%C3%A9e%2C_Le_Petit_Parisien%2C_Num%C3%A9ro_13559%2C_13_December_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Mona_Lisa_Found%2C_La_Joconde_est_Retrouv%C3%A9e%2C_Le_Petit_Parisien%2C_Num%C3%A9ro_13559%2C_13_December_1913.jpg/340px-Mona_Lisa_Found%2C_La_Joconde_est_Retrouv%C3%A9e%2C_Le_Petit_Parisien%2C_Num%C3%A9ro_13559%2C_13_December_1913.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8533" data-file-height="11805" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_Found,_La_Joconde_est_Retrouv%C3%A9e,_Le_Petit_Parisien,_Num%C3%A9ro_13559,_13_December_1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"La Joconde est Retrouvée" ("Mona Lisa is Found"), <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Petit_Parisien" title="Le Petit Parisien">Le Petit Parisien</a></i>, 13 December 1913</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_stolen-1911.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Mona_Lisa_stolen-1911.jpg/170px-Mona_Lisa_stolen-1911.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Mona_Lisa_stolen-1911.jpg/255px-Mona_Lisa_stolen-1911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Mona_Lisa_stolen-1911.jpg/340px-Mona_Lisa_stolen-1911.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="887" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_stolen-1911.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vacant wall in the Louvre's Salon Carré after the painting was stolen in 1911</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Monalisa_uffizi_1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Monalisa_uffizi_1913.jpg/170px-Monalisa_uffizi_1913.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Monalisa_uffizi_1913.jpg/255px-Monalisa_uffizi_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Monalisa_uffizi_1913.jpg/340px-Monalisa_uffizi_1913.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1252" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Monalisa_uffizi_1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Mona Lisa</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Uffizi_Gallery" class="mw-redirect" title="Uffizi Gallery">Uffizi Gallery</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Florence,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Florence, Italy">Florence</a>, 1913. Museum director Giovanni Poggi (right) inspects the painting.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Excelsior,_journal_illustr%C3%A9_quotidien,_La_Joconde_est_Revenue,_1_January_1914.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Excelsior%2C_journal_illustr%C3%A9_quotidien%2C_La_Joconde_est_Revenue%2C_1_January_1914.jpg/170px-Excelsior%2C_journal_illustr%C3%A9_quotidien%2C_La_Joconde_est_Revenue%2C_1_January_1914.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Excelsior%2C_journal_illustr%C3%A9_quotidien%2C_La_Joconde_est_Revenue%2C_1_January_1914.jpg/255px-Excelsior%2C_journal_illustr%C3%A9_quotidien%2C_La_Joconde_est_Revenue%2C_1_January_1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Excelsior%2C_journal_illustr%C3%A9_quotidien%2C_La_Joconde_est_Revenue%2C_1_January_1914.jpg/340px-Excelsior%2C_journal_illustr%C3%A9_quotidien%2C_La_Joconde_est_Revenue%2C_1_January_1914.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1985" data-file-height="2837" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Excelsior,_journal_illustr%C3%A9_quotidien,_La_Joconde_est_Revenue,_1_January_1914.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Excelsior</i>, "La Joconde est Revenue" ("The Mona Lisa has returned"), 1 January 1914</div></div></div>
<p>On 21 August 1911, the painting was <a href="/wiki/Art_theft" title="Art theft">stolen</a> from the Louvre.<sup id="cite_ref-Stoner-PBS_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stoner-PBS-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The missing painting was first noticed the next day by painter <a href="/wiki/Louis_B%C3%A9roud" title="Louis Béroud">Louis Béroud</a>. After some confusion as to whether the painting was being photographed somewhere, the Louvre was closed for a week for investigation.
French poet <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Apollinaire" title="Guillaume Apollinaire">Guillaume Apollinaire</a> came under suspicion and was arrested and imprisoned. Apollinaire implicated his friend <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a>, who was brought in for questioning. Both were later exonerated.<sup id="cite_ref-Scotti2010_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scotti2010-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-monalisa25_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monalisa25-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The real culprit was Louvre employee <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Peruggia" title="Vincenzo Peruggia">Vincenzo Peruggia</a>, who had helped construct the painting's glass case.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> He carried out the theft by entering the building during regular hours, hiding in a broom closet, and walking out with the painting hidden under his coat after the museum had closed.<sup id="cite_ref-louvre1_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-louvre1-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peruggia was an Italian patriot who believed that Leonardo's painting should have been returned to an Italian museum. Peruggia may have been motivated by an associate whose copies of the original would significantly rise in value after the painting's theft.<sup id="cite_ref-lost_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lost-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> After having kept the <i>Mona Lisa</i> in his apartment for two years, Peruggia grew impatient and was caught when he attempted to sell it to directors of the <a href="/wiki/Uffizi_Gallery" class="mw-redirect" title="Uffizi Gallery">Uffizi Gallery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>. It was exhibited in the Uffizi Gallery for over two weeks and returned to the Louvre on 4 January 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Peruggia served six months in prison for the crime and was hailed for his patriotism in Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-monalisa25_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monalisa25-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> A year after the theft, <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Evening_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Saturday Evening Post">Saturday Evening Post</a></i> journalist Karl Decker met an alleged accomplice named <a href="/wiki/Eduardo_de_Valfierno" title="Eduardo de Valfierno">Eduardo de Valfierno</a>, who claimed to have masterminded the theft. Forger <a href="/wiki/Yves_Chaudron" title="Yves Chaudron">Yves Chaudron</a> was to have created six copies of the painting to sell in the US while concealing the location of the original.<sup id="cite_ref-lost_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lost-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Decker published this account of the theft in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 30 December 1956, a rock was thrown at the painting in the Louvre, shattering its glass case and dislodging a speck of pigment near the left elbow, which was later restored.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The painting was protected by glass because a few years earlier a man who claimed to be in love with the painting had cut it with a razor blade and tried to steal it.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
The use of <a href="/wiki/Bulletproof_glass" title="Bulletproof glass">bulletproof glass</a> has shielded the <i>Mona Lisa</i> from subsequent attacks. In April 1974, while the painting was on display at the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a>, a woman sprayed it with red paint as a protest against that museum's failure to provide access for disabled people.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> On 2 August 2009, a Russian woman, distraught over being denied French citizenship, threw a ceramic teacup purchased at the Louvre; the vessel shattered against the glass enclosure.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In both cases, the painting was undamaged.
</p><p>In recent decades, the painting has been temporarily moved to accommodate renovations to the Louvre on three occasions: between 1992 and 1995, from 2001 to 2005, and again in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> A new queuing system introduced in 2019 reduces the amount of time museum visitors have to wait in line to see the painting. After going through the queue, a group has about 30 seconds to see the painting.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_(dite_La_Joconde)_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_(right_landscape).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28right_landscape%29.jpg/170px-Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28right_landscape%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28right_landscape%29.jpg/255px-Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28right_landscape%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28right_landscape%29.jpg/340px-Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28right_landscape%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_(dite_La_Joconde)_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_(right_landscape).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of the background (right side)</div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Mona Lisa</i> bears a strong resemblance to many Renaissance depictions of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>, who was at that time seen as an ideal for womanhood.<sup id="cite_ref-Zollner1_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zollner1-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
The woman sits markedly upright in a "pozzetto" armchair with her arms folded, a sign of her reserved posture. Her gaze is fixed on the observer.  The woman appears alive to an unusual extent, which Leonardo achieved by his method of not drawing outlines (<i><a href="/wiki/Sfumato" title="Sfumato">sfumato</a></i>). The soft blending creates an ambiguous mood "mainly in two features: the corners of the mouth, and the corners of the eyes".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The depiction of the sitter in three-quarter profile is similar to late 15th-century works by <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_di_Credi" title="Lorenzo di Credi">Lorenzo di Credi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agnolo_di_Domenico_del_Mazziere" title="Agnolo di Domenico del Mazziere">Agnolo di Domenico del Mazziere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zollner1_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zollner1-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Z%C3%B6llner" title="Frank Zöllner">Zöllner</a> notes that the sitter's general position can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Flemish_painting" title="Flemish painting">Flemish</a> models and that "in particular the vertical slices of columns at both sides of the panel had precedents in Flemish portraiture."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Woods-Marsden cites <a href="/wiki/Hans_Memling" title="Hans Memling">Hans Memling</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Portinari_Triptych" title="Benedetto Portinari Triptych">portrait of Benedetto Portinari</a> (1487) or Italian imitations such as <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Davide_Ghirlandaio_Retrato_de_dama_de_perfil_Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie,_Berl%C3%ADn.jpg" class="extiw" title="commons:File:Davide Ghirlandaio Retrato de dama de perfil Gemäldegalerie, Berlín.jpg">Sebastiano Mainardi's pendant portraits</a> for the use of a <i><a href="/wiki/Loggia" title="Loggia">loggia</a></i>, which has the effect of mediating between the sitter and the distant landscape, a feature missing from Leonardo's earlier portrait of <i><a href="/wiki/Ginevra_de%27_Benci" title="Ginevra de&#39; Benci">Ginevra de' Benci</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Marsden77_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marsden77-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_(dite_La_Joconde)_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_(hands).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28hands%29.jpg/220px-Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28hands%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28hands%29.jpg/330px-Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28hands%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28hands%29.jpg/440px-Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_%28dite_La_Joconde%29_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_%28hands%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="758" data-file-height="569" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_di_ser_Piero_da_Vinci_-_Portrait_de_Mona_Lisa_(dite_La_Joconde)_-_Louvre_779_-_Detail_(hands).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of Lisa's hands, her right hand resting on her left. Leonardo chose this gesture rather than a wedding ring to depict Lisa as a virtuous woman and faithful wife.<sup id="cite_ref-Z12_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Z12-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div><p>The painting was one of the first portraits to depict the sitter in front of an imaginary landscape, and Leonardo was one of the first painters to use <a href="/wiki/Aerial_perspective" title="Aerial perspective">aerial perspective</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The enigmatic woman is portrayed seated in what appears to be an open loggia with dark pillar bases on either side. Behind her, a vast landscape recedes to icy mountains. Winding paths and a distant bridge give only the slightest indications of human presence. Leonardo has chosen to place the horizon line not at the neck, as he did with <i>Ginevra de' Benci</i>, but on a level with the eyes, thus linking the figure with the landscape and emphasizing the mysterious nature of the painting.<sup id="cite_ref-Marsden77_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marsden77-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Mona Lisa</i> has no clearly visible eyebrows or eyelashes. Some researchers claim that it was common at this time for genteel women to pluck these hairs, as they were considered unsightly.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, French engineer Pascal Cotte announced that his ultra-high resolution scans of the painting provide evidence that <i>Mona Lisa</i> was originally painted with eyelashes and with visible eyebrows, but that these had gradually disappeared over time, perhaps as a result of overcleaning.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Cotte discovered the painting had been reworked several times, with changes made to the size of the Mona Lisa's face and the direction of her gaze. He also found that in one layer the subject was depicted wearing numerous hairpins and a headdress adorned with pearls which was later scrubbed out and overpainted.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There has been much speculation regarding the painting's model and landscape. For example, Leonardo probably painted his model faithfully since her beauty is not seen as being among the best, "even when measured by late quattrocento (15th century) or even twenty-first century standards."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Some art historians in Eastern art, such as <a href="/wiki/Yukio_Yashiro" title="Yukio Yashiro">Yukio Yashiro</a>, argue that the landscape in the background of the picture was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_paintings" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese paintings">Chinese paintings</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Salgueiro_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salgueiro-92">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> but this thesis has been contested for lack of clear evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-Salgueiro_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salgueiro-92">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research in 2003 by Professor <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Livingstone" title="Margaret Livingstone">Margaret Livingstone</a> of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> said that Mona Lisa's smile disappears when observed with direct vision, known as <a href="/wiki/Foveal" title="Foveal">foveal</a>. Because of the way the human eye processes visual information, it is less suited to pick up shadows directly; however, <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_vision" title="Peripheral vision">peripheral vision</a> can pick up shadows well.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research in 2008 by a geomorphology professor at <a href="/wiki/Urbino_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Urbino University">Urbino University</a> and an artist-photographer revealed likenesses of <i>Mona Lisa</i><span class="nowrap">&#39;</span>s landscapes to some views in the <a href="/wiki/Montefeltro" title="Montefeltro">Montefeltro</a> region in the Italian provinces of <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pesaro_and_Urbino" title="Province of Pesaro and Urbino">Pesaro and Urbino</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Rimini" title="Province of Rimini">Rimini</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span></h2>
<p>The <i>Mona Lisa</i> has survived for more than 500 years, and an international commission convened in 1952 noted that "the picture is in a remarkable state of preservation."<sup id="cite_ref-mohen_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohen-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> This is partly due to a variety of conservation treatments the painting has undergone. A detailed analysis in 1933 by Madame de Gironde revealed that earlier restorers had "acted with a great deal of restraint."<sup id="cite_ref-mohen_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohen-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, applications of <a href="/wiki/Varnish" title="Varnish">varnish</a> made to the painting had darkened even by the end of the 16th century, and an aggressive 1809 cleaning and revarnishing removed some of the uppermost portion of the paint layer, resulting in a washed-out appearance to the face of the figure. Despite the treatments, the <i>Mona Lisa</i> has been well cared for throughout its history, and although the panel's warping caused the curators "some worry",<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> the 2004–05 conservation team was optimistic about the future of the work.<sup id="cite_ref-mohen_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohen-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poplar_panel">Poplar panel</span></h3>
<p>At some point, the <i>Mona Lisa</i> was removed from its original frame. The unconstrained poplar panel warped freely with changes in humidity, and as a result, a crack developed near the top of the panel, extending down to the hairline of the figure. In the mid-18th century to early 19th century, two butterfly-shaped walnut braces were inserted into the back of the panel to a depth of about one third the thickness of the panel. This intervention was skilfully executed, and successfully stabilized the crack. Sometime between 1888 and 1905, or perhaps during the picture's theft, the upper brace fell out. A later restorer glued and lined the resulting socket and crack with cloth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The picture is kept under strict, climate-controlled conditions in its bulletproof glass case. The humidity is maintained at 50%&#160;±10%, and the temperature is maintained between 18 and 21&#160;°C. To compensate for fluctuations in relative humidity, the case is supplemented with a bed of <a href="/wiki/Silica_gel" title="Silica gel">silica gel</a> treated to provide 55% relative humidity.<sup id="cite_ref-mohen_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohen-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frame">Frame</span></h3>
<p>Because the <i>Mona Lisa</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s poplar support expands and contracts with changes in humidity, the picture has experienced some warping. In response to warping and swelling experienced during its storage during World War II, and to prepare the picture for an exhibit to honour the anniversary of Leonardo's 500th birthday, the <i>Mona Lisa</i> was fitted in 1951 with a flexible oak frame with beech crosspieces. This flexible frame, which is used in addition to the decorative frame described below, exerts pressure on the panel to keep it from warping further. In 1970, the beech <a href="/wiki/Cross_brace" title="Cross brace">crosspieces</a> were switched to maple after it was found that the <a href="/wiki/Beechwood" class="mw-redirect" title="Beechwood">beechwood</a> had been infested with insects. In 2004–05, a conservation and study team replaced the maple crosspieces with sycamore ones, and an additional metal crosspiece was added for scientific measurement of the panel's warp.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <i>Mona Lisa</i> has had many different decorative frames in its history, owing to changes in taste over the centuries. In 1909, the art collector <a href="/w/index.php?title=Martine-Marie-Pol_de_B%C3%A9hague&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Martine-Marie-Pol de Béhague (page does not exist)">Comtesse de Béhague</a> gave the portrait its current frame,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> a Renaissance-era work consistent with the historical period of the <i>Mona Lisa</i>. The edges of the painting have been trimmed at least once in its history to fit the picture into various frames, but no part of the original paint layer has been trimmed.<sup id="cite_ref-mohen_45-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohen-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cleaning_and_touch-up">Cleaning and touch-up</span></h3>
<p>The first and most extensive recorded cleaning, revarnishing, and touch-up of the <i>Mona Lisa</i> was an 1809 wash and revarnishing undertaken by Jean-Marie Hooghstoel, who was responsible for restoration of paintings for the galleries of the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Napol%C3%A9on" class="mw-redirect" title="Musée Napoléon">Musée Napoléon</a>. The work involved cleaning with spirits, touch-up of colour, and revarnishing the painting. In 1906, Louvre restorer Eugène Denizard performed watercolour retouches on areas of the paint layer disturbed by the crack in the panel. Denizard also retouched the edges of the picture with varnish, to mask areas that had been covered initially by an older frame. In 1913, when the painting was recovered after its theft, Denizard was again called upon to work on the <i>Mona Lisa</i>. Denizard was directed to clean the picture without <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>, and to lightly touch up several scratches to the painting with watercolour. In 1952, the varnish layer over the background in the painting was evened out. After the second 1956 attack, restorer Jean-Gabriel Goulinat was directed to touch up the damage to <i>Mona Lisa</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s left elbow with watercolour.<sup id="cite_ref-mohen_45-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohen-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1977, a new insect infestation was discovered in the back of the panel as a result of crosspieces installed to keep the painting from warping. This was treated on the spot with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">carbon tetrachloride</a>, and later with an <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a> treatment. In 1985, the spot was again treated with carbon tetrachloride as a preventive measure.<sup id="cite_ref-mohen_45-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohen-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Display">Display</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MonaLisaShield.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/MonaLisaShield.jpg/220px-MonaLisaShield.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/MonaLisaShield.jpg/330px-MonaLisaShield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/MonaLisaShield.jpg/440px-MonaLisaShield.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MonaLisaShield.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Mona Lisa</i> behind <a href="/wiki/Bulletproof_glass" title="Bulletproof glass">bulletproof glass</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>On 6 April 2005—following a period of curatorial maintenance, recording, and analysis—the painting was moved to a new location within the museum's Salle des États. It is displayed in a purpose-built, climate-controlled enclosure behind bulletproof glass.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Since 2005 the painting has been illuminated by an <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lamp</a>, and in 2013 a new 20 watt LED lamp was installed, specially designed for this painting. The lamp has a <a href="/wiki/Colour_Rendering_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Colour Rendering Index">Colour Rendering Index</a> up to 98, and minimizes <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation which could otherwise degrade the painting.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The renovation of the gallery where the painting now resides was financed by the Japanese broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Nippon_TV" title="Nippon TV">Nippon Television</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> About 6&#160;million people view the painting at the Louvre each year.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-Faces_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-Faces-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fame">Fame</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crowd_looking_at_the_Mona_Lisa_at_the_Louvre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Crowd_looking_at_the_Mona_Lisa_at_the_Louvre.jpg/300px-Crowd_looking_at_the_Mona_Lisa_at_the_Louvre.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Crowd_looking_at_the_Mona_Lisa_at_the_Louvre.jpg/450px-Crowd_looking_at_the_Mona_Lisa_at_the_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Crowd_looking_at_the_Mona_Lisa_at_the_Louvre.jpg/600px-Crowd_looking_at_the_Mona_Lisa_at_the_Louvre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5760" data-file-height="3240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crowd_looking_at_the_Mona_Lisa_at_the_Louvre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2014: <i>Mona Lisa</i> is among the greatest attractions in the Louvre.</div></div></div>
<p>Today the <i>Mona Lisa</i> is considered the most famous painting in the world, but until the 20th century it was simply one among many highly regarded artworks.<sup id="cite_ref-riding_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riding-101">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
Once part of <a href="/wiki/King_Francis_I_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="King Francis I of France">King Francis I of France</a>'s collection, the <i>Mona Lisa</i> was among the first artworks to be exhibited in the Louvre, which became a national museum after the French Revolution. Leonardo began to be revered as a genius, and the painting's popularity grew in the mid-19th century when French intelligentsia praised it as mysterious and a representation of the <a href="/wiki/Femme_fatale" title="Femme fatale">femme fatale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Baedeker" title="Baedeker">Baedeker</a> guide in 1878 called it "the most celebrated work of Leonardo in the Louvre",<sup id="cite_ref-Sassoon_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sassoon-103">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> but the painting was known more by the <a href="/wiki/Intelligentsia" title="Intelligentsia">intelligentsia</a> than the general public.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK,_Marie-Madeleine_Lioux,_Andr%C3%A9_Malraux,_Jackie,_L.B._Johnson,_unveiling_Mona_Lisa_at_National_Gallery_of_Art.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/JFK%2C_Marie-Madeleine_Lioux%2C_Andr%C3%A9_Malraux%2C_Jackie%2C_L.B._Johnson%2C_unveiling_Mona_Lisa_at_National_Gallery_of_Art.png/220px-JFK%2C_Marie-Madeleine_Lioux%2C_Andr%C3%A9_Malraux%2C_Jackie%2C_L.B._Johnson%2C_unveiling_Mona_Lisa_at_National_Gallery_of_Art.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/JFK%2C_Marie-Madeleine_Lioux%2C_Andr%C3%A9_Malraux%2C_Jackie%2C_L.B._Johnson%2C_unveiling_Mona_Lisa_at_National_Gallery_of_Art.png/330px-JFK%2C_Marie-Madeleine_Lioux%2C_Andr%C3%A9_Malraux%2C_Jackie%2C_L.B._Johnson%2C_unveiling_Mona_Lisa_at_National_Gallery_of_Art.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/JFK%2C_Marie-Madeleine_Lioux%2C_Andr%C3%A9_Malraux%2C_Jackie%2C_L.B._Johnson%2C_unveiling_Mona_Lisa_at_National_Gallery_of_Art.png/440px-JFK%2C_Marie-Madeleine_Lioux%2C_Andr%C3%A9_Malraux%2C_Jackie%2C_L.B._Johnson%2C_unveiling_Mona_Lisa_at_National_Gallery_of_Art.png 2x" data-file-width="2598" data-file-height="2305" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK,_Marie-Madeleine_Lioux,_Andr%C3%A9_Malraux,_Jackie,_L.B._Johnson,_unveiling_Mona_Lisa_at_National_Gallery_of_Art.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_Malraux" title="Madeleine Malraux">Madeleine Malraux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Malraux" title="André Malraux">André Malraux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacqueline Kennedy">Jacqueline Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> at the unveiling of the <i>Mona Lisa</i> at the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Art" title="National Gallery of Art">National Gallery of Art</a> during its visit to Washington D.C., 8 January 1963</div></div></div>
<p>The 1911 theft of the <i>Mona Lisa</i> and its subsequent return was reported worldwide, leading to a massive increase in public recognition of the painting. During the 20th century it was an object for mass reproduction, merchandising, lampooning and speculation, and was claimed to have been reproduced in "300 paintings and 2,000 advertisements".<sup id="cite_ref-Sassoon_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sassoon-103">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Mona Lisa</i> was regarded as "just another Leonardo until early last century, when the scandal of the painting's theft from the Louvre and subsequent return kept a spotlight on it over several years."<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From December 1962 to March 1963, the French government lent it to the United States to be displayed in New York City and Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stolow1987_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolow1987-106">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> It was shipped on the new ocean liner <a href="/wiki/SS_France_(1961)" class="mw-redirect" title="SS France (1961)">SS <i>France</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In New York, an estimated 1.7&#160;million people queued "in order to cast a glance at the Mona Lisa for 20 seconds or so."<sup id="cite_ref-Sassoon_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sassoon-103">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> While exhibited in the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, the painting was nearly drenched in water because of a faulty sprinkler, but the painting's bullet-proof glass case protected it.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1974, the painting was exhibited in Tokyo and Moscow.<sup id="cite_ref-Bohm-Duchen2001_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohm-Duchen2001-109">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, 9.3&#160;million people visited the Louvre.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Former director <a href="/wiki/Henri_Loyrette" title="Henri Loyrette">Henri Loyrette</a> reckoned that "80&#160;percent of the people only want to see the <i>Mona Lisa</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_worth">Financial worth</span></h3>
<p>Before the 1962–1963 tour, the painting was assessed for insurance at $100&#160;million (equivalent to <span class="nowrap">$650 million</span> in 2018), making it, in practice, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_expensive_paintings#List_of_highest_prices_paid" title="List of most expensive paintings">most valued painting</a> in the world. The insurance was not purchased; instead, more was spent on security.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, a <a href="/wiki/France_24" title="France 24">France 24</a> article suggested that the painting could be sold to help ease the national debt, although it was noted that the <i>Mona Lisa</i> and other such art works were prohibited from being sold due to French heritage law, which states that "Collections held in museums that belong to public bodies are considered public property and cannot be otherwise."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
