<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa_replicas_and_reinterpretations" title="Mona Lisa replicas and reinterpretations">Mona Lisa replicas and reinterpretations</a></div>
<p>The <i>Mona Lisa</i> began influencing contemporary <a href="/wiki/Florentine_painting" title="Florentine painting">Florentine painting</a> even before its completion.  <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, who had been to Leonardo's workshop several times, promptly used elements of the portrait's composition and format in several of his works, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Young_Woman_with_Unicorn" title="Young Woman with Unicorn">Young Woman with Unicorn</a></i> (c.&#160;1506),<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_Maddalena_Doni" title="Portrait of Maddalena Doni">Portrait of Maddalena Doni</a></i> (c.&#160;1506).<sup id="cite_ref-Reynal_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynal-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Later paintings by Raphael, such as <i lang="it" title="Italian language text"><a href="/wiki/La_velata" title="La velata">La velata</a></i> (1515–16) and <i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_Baldassare_Castiglione" title="Portrait of Baldassare Castiglione">Portrait of Baldassare Castiglione</a></i> (c.&#160;1514–15), continued to borrow from Leonardo's painting. Zollner states that "None of Leonardo's works would exert more influence upon the evolution of the genre than the <i>Mona Lisa</i>.  It became the definitive example of the Renaissance portrait and perhaps for this reason is seen not just as the likeness of a real person, but also as the embodiment of an ideal."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early commentators such as <a href="/wiki/Vasari" class="mw-redirect" title="Vasari">Vasari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_F%C3%A9libien" title="André Félibien">André Félibien</a> praised the picture for its <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realism</a>, but by the Victorian era, writers began to regard the <i>Mona Lisa</i> as imbued with a sense of mystery and <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romance</a>.  In 1859, <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9ophile_Gautier" title="Théophile Gautier">Théophile Gautier</a> wrote that the <i>Mona Lisa</i> was a "sphinx of beauty who smiles so mysteriously" and that "Beneath the form expressed one feels a thought that is vague, infinite, inexpressible. One is moved, troubled ... repressed desires, hopes that drive one to despair, stir painfully."  <a href="/wiki/Walter_Pater" title="Walter Pater">Walter Pater</a>'s famous essay of 1869 described the sitter as "older than the rocks among which she sits; like the vampire, she has been dead many times, and learned the secrets of the grave; and has been a diver in the deep seas, and keeps their fallen day about her."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the early 20th century, some critics started to feel the painting had become a repository for subjective exegeses and theories.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Upon the painting's theft in 1911, Renaissance historian <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Berenson" title="Bernard Berenson">Bernard Berenson</a> admitted that it had "simply become an incubus, and [he] was glad to be rid of her."<sup id="cite_ref-auto_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean_Metzinger" title="Jean Metzinger">Jean Metzinger</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Le_go%C3%BBter_(Tea_Time)" title="Le goûter (Tea Time)">Le goûter (Tea Time)</a></i> was exhibited at the 1911 <a href="/wiki/Salon_d%27Automne" title="Salon d&#39;Automne">Salon d'Automne</a> and was sarcastically described as "la Joconde à la cuiller" (Mona Lisa with a spoon) by art critic <a href="/wiki/Louis_Vauxcelles" title="Louis Vauxcelles">Louis Vauxcelles</a> on the front page of <a href="/wiki/Gil_Blas" title="Gil Blas">Gil Blas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vauxcelles_1911_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vauxcelles_1911-119">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Salmon" title="André Salmon">André Salmon</a> subsequently described the painting as "The Mona Lisa of Cubism".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean_Metzinger,_Le_go%C3%BBter,_Tea_Time,_1911,_75.9_x_70.2_cm,_Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/Jean_Metzinger%2C_Le_go%C3%BBter%2C_Tea_Time%2C_1911%2C_75.9_x_70.2_cm%2C_Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art.jpg/220px-Jean_Metzinger%2C_Le_go%C3%BBter%2C_Tea_Time%2C_1911%2C_75.9_x_70.2_cm%2C_Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/Jean_Metzinger%2C_Le_go%C3%BBter%2C_Tea_Time%2C_1911%2C_75.9_x_70.2_cm%2C_Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art.jpg/330px-Jean_Metzinger%2C_Le_go%C3%BBter%2C_Tea_Time%2C_1911%2C_75.9_x_70.2_cm%2C_Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/Jean_Metzinger%2C_Le_go%C3%BBter%2C_Tea_Time%2C_1911%2C_75.9_x_70.2_cm%2C_Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art.jpg/440px-Jean_Metzinger%2C_Le_go%C3%BBter%2C_Tea_Time%2C_1911%2C_75.9_x_70.2_cm%2C_Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1473" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean_Metzinger,_Le_go%C3%BBter,_Tea_Time,_1911,_75.9_x_70.2_cm,_Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jean_Metzinger" title="Jean Metzinger">Jean Metzinger</a>, 1911, <i><a href="/wiki/Le_go%C3%BBter_(Tea_Time)" title="Le goûter (Tea Time)">Le goûter (Tea Time)</a></i>, oil on canvas, 75.9 x 70.2&#160;cm, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art" title="Philadelphia Museum of Art">Philadelphia Museum of Art</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> art world has made note of the <i>Mona Lisa</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s undeniable popularity. Because of the painting's overwhelming stature, <a href="/wiki/Dada" title="Dada">Dadaists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">Surrealists</a> often produce modifications and <a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">caricatures</a>. In 1883, <i>Le rire,</i> an image of a <i>Mona Lisa</i> smoking a pipe, by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sapeck&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sapeck (page does not exist)">Sapeck</a> (Eugène Bataille), was shown at the "<a href="/wiki/Incoherents" title="Incoherents">Incoherents</a>" show in Paris. In 1919, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Duchamp" title="Marcel Duchamp">Marcel Duchamp</a>, one of the most influential modern artists, created <i><a href="/wiki/L.H.O.O.Q." title="L.H.O.O.Q.">L.H.O.O.Q.</a></i>, a <i>Mona Lisa</i> parody made by adorning a cheap reproduction with a moustache and goatee. Duchamp added an inscription, which when read out loud in French sounds like "Elle a chaud au cul" meaning: "she has a hot ass", implying the woman in the painting is in a state of sexual excitement and intended as a <a href="/wiki/Humor_in_Freud" title="Humor in Freud">Freudian joke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Rhonda_Roland_Shearer" title="Rhonda Roland Shearer">Rhonda R. Shearer</a>, the apparent reproduction is in fact a copy partly modelled on Duchamp's own face.<sup id="cite_ref-asrl_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asrl-124">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD" title="Salvador Dalí">Salvador Dalí</a>, famous for his surrealist work, painted <i>Self portrait as Mona Lisa</i> in 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a> created <a href="/wiki/Serigraphy" class="mw-redirect" title="Serigraphy">serigraph</a> prints of multiple <i>Mona Lisa</i>s, called <i>Thirty Are Better than One</i>, following the painting's visit to the United States in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The French urban artist known pseudonymously as <a href="/wiki/Invader_(artist)" title="Invader (artist)">Invader</a> has created versions of the <i>Mona Lisa</i> on city walls in Paris and Tokyo using a mosaic style.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> A 2014 <i>New Yorker</i> magazine cartoon parodies the supposed enigma of the <i>Mona Lisa</i> smile in an animation showing progressively more maniacal smiles.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_versions_and_copies">Early versions and copies</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_lisa_Prado_PERSPECTIVA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mona_lisa_Prado_PERSPECTIVA.jpg/100px-Mona_lisa_Prado_PERSPECTIVA.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mona_lisa_Prado_PERSPECTIVA.jpg/150px-Mona_lisa_Prado_PERSPECTIVA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mona_lisa_Prado_PERSPECTIVA.jpg/200px-Mona_lisa_Prado_PERSPECTIVA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="976" data-file-height="1448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_lisa_Prado_PERSPECTIVA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Perspective of <i>Mona Lisa</i>, Museo del Prado</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_Louvre_PERSPECTIVA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Mona_Lisa_Louvre_PERSPECTIVA.jpg/100px-Mona_Lisa_Louvre_PERSPECTIVA.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Mona_Lisa_Louvre_PERSPECTIVA.jpg/150px-Mona_Lisa_Louvre_PERSPECTIVA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Mona_Lisa_Louvre_PERSPECTIVA.jpg/200px-Mona_Lisa_Louvre_PERSPECTIVA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1044" data-file-height="1432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_Louvre_PERSPECTIVA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Perspective of <i>Mona Lisa</i>, Louvre</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prado_Museum_La_Gioconda"><i>Prado Museum La Gioconda</i></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa_(Prado%27s_version)" title="Mona Lisa (Prado&#39;s version)">Mona Lisa (Prado's version)</a></div>
<p>A version of <i>Mona Lisa</i> known as <i lang="es" title="Spanish language text">Mujer de mano de Leonardo Abince</i> ("Woman by Leonardo da Vinci's hand") held in Madrid's <a href="/wiki/Museo_del_Prado" title="Museo del Prado">Museo del Prado</a> was for centuries considered to be a work by Leonardo. However, since its restoration in 2012 it is considered to have been executed by one of <a href="/wiki/Leonardeschi" title="Leonardeschi">Leonardo's pupils</a> in his studio at the same time as <i>Mona Lisa</i> was being painted.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The Prado's conclusion that that the painting is probably by <a href="/wiki/Sala%C3%AC" title="Salaì">Salaì</a> (1480–1524) or by <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Melzi" title="Francesco Melzi">Melzi</a> (1493–1572) has been called into question by others.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The restored painting is from a slightly different perspective than the original <i>Mona Lisa</i>, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Speculations_about_Mona_Lisa" title="Speculations about Mona Lisa">speculation</a> that it is part of the world's first <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Stereoscopic">stereoscopic</a> pair.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> However, a more recent report has demonstrated that this stereoscopic pair in fact gives no reliable stereoscopic depth.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isleworth_Mona_Lisa"><i>Isleworth Mona Lisa</i></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isleworth_Mona_Lisa" title="Isleworth Mona Lisa">Isleworth Mona Lisa</a></div>
<p>A version of the <i>Mona Lisa</i> known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Isleworth_Mona_Lisa" title="Isleworth Mona Lisa">Isleworth Mona Lisa</a></i> and also known as the <i>Earlier Mona Lisa</i> was first bought by an English nobleman in 1778 and was rediscovered in 1913 by <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Blaker" title="Hugh Blaker">Hugh Blaker</a>, an art connoisseur. The painting was presented to the media in 2012 by the Mona Lisa Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> It is a painting of the same subject as Leonardo da Vinci's <i>Mona Lisa</i>. The painting is claimed by a majority of experts to be mostly an original work of Leonardo dating from the early 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Lorusso_Natali_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorusso_Natali-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Asmus_Parfenov_Elford_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asmus_Parfenov_Elford-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Isbouts_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Isbouts-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boudin_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boudin-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Other experts, including <a href="/wiki/Frank_Z%C3%B6llner" title="Frank Zöllner">Zöllner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Kemp_(art_historian)" title="Martin Kemp (art historian)">Kemp</a>, deny the attribution.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa_(copy,_Thalwil,_Switzerland).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mona_Lisa_%28copy%2C_Thalwil%2C_Switzerland%29.JPG/147px-Mona_Lisa_%28copy%2C_Thalwil%2C_Switzerland%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="147" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mona_Lisa_%28copy%2C_Thalwil%2C_Switzerland%29.JPG/221px-Mona_Lisa_%28copy%2C_Thalwil%2C_Switzerland%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mona_Lisa_%28copy%2C_Thalwil%2C_Switzerland%29.JPG/294px-Mona_Lisa_%28copy%2C_Thalwil%2C_Switzerland%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2019" data-file-height="2744" /></a></div></div>
